<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Microorganism</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Microbe" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Microbe_(disambiguation)" title="Microbe (disambiguation)">Microbe (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E_coli_at_10000x,_original.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1889" data-file-width="2598" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg/250px-E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg/375px-E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg/500px-E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E_coli_at_10000x,_original.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)#Microbial_colonies" title="Colony (biology)">cluster</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> magnified 10,000 times</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>microorganism</b>, or <b>microbe</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[a]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Microscopic_scale" title="Microscopic scale">microscopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a>, which may exist in its <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">single-celled</a> form or in a <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)#Microbial_colonies" title="Colony (biology)">colony of cells</a>.
</p><p>The possible existence of unseen microbial life was suspected from ancient times, such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_scriptures" title="Jain scriptures">Jain scriptures</a> from 6th century BC India and the 1st century BC book <i>On Agriculture</i> by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Terentius_Varro" title="Marcus Terentius Varro">Marcus Terentius Varro</a>. <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">Microbiology</a>, the scientific study of microorganisms, began with their observation under the <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a> in the 1670s by <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a>. In the 1850s, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> found that microorganisms caused <a href="/wiki/Food_spoilage" title="Food spoilage">food spoilage</a>, debunking the theory of <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a>. In the 1880s <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> discovered that microorganisms caused the diseases <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>.
</p><p>Microorganisms include all <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular organisms</a> and so are extremely diverse. Of the <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three domains of life</a> identified by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">Carl Woese</a>, all of the <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> are microorganisms. These were previously grouped together in the <a href="/wiki/Two-empire_system" title="Two-empire system">two domain system</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes">Prokaryotes</a>, the other being the eukaryotes. The third domain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a> includes all <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> and many unicellular <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoans</a>. Some protists are related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">animals</a> and some to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_plants" title="Green plants">green plants</a>. Many of the multicellular organisms are microscopic, namely <a href="/wiki/Micro-animal" title="Micro-animal">micro-animals</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, but these are not discussed here.
</p><p>They live in almost every <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographic_pole" title="Geographic pole">poles</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea" title="Deep sea">deep sea</a>. Some are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extremophiles" title="Extremophiles">adapted to extremes</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperthermophiles" title="Hyperthermophiles">very hot</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psychrophile" title="Psychrophile">very cold conditions</a>, others to <a href="/wiki/Piezophile" title="Piezophile">high pressure</a> and a few such as <i><a href="/wiki/Deinococcus_radiodurans" title="Deinococcus radiodurans">Deinococcus radiodurans</a></i> to <a href="/wiki/Radioresistance" title="Radioresistance">high radiation</a> environments. Microorganisms also make up the <a href="/wiki/Microbiota" title="Microbiota">microbiota</a> found in and on all multicellular organisms. A December 2017 report stated that 3.45-billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> rocks once contained microorganisms, the earliest direct evidence of life on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WU-20171218_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-WU-20171218-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-2017_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-2017-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbes_in_human_culture" title="Microbes in human culture">Microbes are important in human culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a> in many ways, serving to <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_in_food_processing" title="Fermentation in food processing">ferment foods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">treat sewage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">produce fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioactive_compound" title="Bioactive compound">bioactive compounds</a>. They are essential tools in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> as <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a> and have been put to use in <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological warfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">bioterrorism</a>. They are a vital <a href="/wiki/Soil_microbiology" title="Soil microbiology">component of fertile soils</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a> microorganisms make up the <a href="/wiki/Human_microbiota" title="Human microbiota">human microbiota</a> including the essential <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a>. They are the <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> responsible for many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> and as such are the target of <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene measures</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microorganism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_biology" title="History of biology">History of biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microbiology#History" title="Microbiology">Microbiology § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_(1632-1723)._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2547" data-file-width="2148" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg/170px-Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg/255px-Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg/340px-Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_(1632-1723)._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> was the first to study microorganisms, using simple <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopes</a> of his own design.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spallanzani.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1043" data-file-width="817" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Spallanzani.jpg/170px-Spallanzani.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Spallanzani.jpg/255px-Spallanzani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Spallanzani.jpg/340px-Spallanzani.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spallanzani.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lazzaro_Spallanzani" title="Lazzaro Spallanzani">Lazzaro Spallanzani</a> showed that boiling a broth stopped it from decaying.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_precursors">Ancient precursors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microorganism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient precursors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The possible existence of microorganisms was discussed for many centuries before their discovery in the 17th century. By the fifth century BC, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jains</a> of present-day India postulated the existence of tiny organisms called <a href="/wiki/Nigoda" title="Nigoda">nigodas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JDL_2013_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-JDL_2013-4">[3]</a></sup> These nigodas are said to be born in clusters; they live everywhere, including the bodies of plants, animals, and people; and their life lasts only for fraction of a second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> According to the Jain leader Mahavira, the humans destroy these nigodas on a massive scale, when they eat, breathe, sit and move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JDL_2013_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-JDL_2013-4">[3]</a></sup> Many modern Jains assert that Mahavira's teachings presage the existence of microorganisms as discovered by modern science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known idea to indicate the possibility of diseases spreading by yet unseen organisms was that of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Terentius_Varro" title="Marcus Terentius Varro">Marcus Terentius Varro</a> in a 1st-century BC book titled <i>On Agriculture</i> in which he called the unseen creatures <a href="/wiki/Animalcule" title="Animalcule">animalcules</a>, and warns against locating a homestead near a swamp:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varro_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varro-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>… and because there are bred certain minute creatures that cannot be seen by the eyes, which float in the air and enter the body through the mouth and nose and they cause serious diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varro_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Varro-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i> (1020), <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> suggested that <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> and other diseases might be contagious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern">Early modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microorganism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Akshamsaddin" title="Akshamsaddin">Akshamsaddin</a> (Turkish scientist) mentioned the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbe</a> in his work <i>Maddat ul-Hayat</i> (The Material of Life) about two centuries prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonie_Van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie Van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie Van Leeuwenhoek</a>'s discovery through experimentation:
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">It is incorrect to assume that diseases appear one by one in humans. Disease infects by spreading from one person to another. This infection occurs through seeds that are so small they cannot be seen but are alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1546_in_science#Medicine" title="1546 in science">1546</a>, <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Fracastoro" title="Girolamo Fracastoro">Girolamo Fracastoro</a> proposed that <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diseases" title="Diseases">diseases</a> were caused by transferable seedlike entities that could transmit infection by direct or indirect contact, or even without contact over long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonie_Van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie Van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie Van Leeuwenhoek</a> is considered to be the <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_considered_father_or_mother_of_a_scientific_field" title="List of people considered father or mother of a scientific field">father of microbiology</a>. He was the first in <a href="/wiki/1673_in_science" title="1673 in science">1673</a> to discover, observe, describe, study and conduct scientific experiments with microoorganisms, using simple single-lensed <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopes</a> of his own design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NickLane_RS_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NickLane_RS-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>, a contemporary of Leeuwenhoek, also used <a href="/wiki/Microscopy" title="Microscopy">microscopy</a> to observe microbial life in the form of the fruiting bodies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mould" title="Mould">moulds</a>. In his <a href="/wiki/1665#July–December" title="1665">1665</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/Micrographia" title="Micrographia">Micrographia</a></i>, he made drawings of studies, and he coined the term <i><a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microorganism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2532" data-file-width="2082" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg/170px-Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg/255px-Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg/340px-Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> showed that Spallanzani's findings held even if air could enter through a filter that kept particles out.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> (1822–1895) exposed boiled broths to the air, in vessels that contained a filter to prevent particles from passing through to the <a href="/wiki/Growth_medium" title="Growth medium">growth medium</a>, and also in vessels without a filter, but with air allowed in via a curved tube so dust particles would settle and not come in contact with the broth. By boiling the broth beforehand, Pasteur ensured that no microorganisms survived within the broths at the beginning of his experiment. Nothing grew in the broths in the course of Pasteur's experiment. This meant that the living organisms that grew in such broths came from outside, as <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> on dust, rather than spontaneously generated within the broth. Thus, Pasteur dealt the death blow to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a> and supported the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Koch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3124" data-file-width="2519" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Robert_Koch.jpg/170px-Robert_Koch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Robert_Koch.jpg/255px-Robert_Koch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Robert_Koch.jpg/340px-Robert_Koch.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Koch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> showed that microorganisms caused <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1876, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> (1843–1910) established that microorganisms can cause disease. He found that the blood of cattle which were infected with <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> always had large numbers of <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i>. Koch found that he could transmit anthrax from one animal to another by taking a small sample of blood from the infected animal and injecting it into a healthy one, and this caused the healthy animal to become sick. He also found that he could grow the bacteria in a nutrient broth, then inject it into a healthy animal, and cause illness. Based on these experiments, he devised criteria for establishing a causal link between a microorganism and a disease and these are now known as <a href="/wiki/Koch%27s_postulates" title="Koch's postulates">Koch's postulates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Although these postulates cannot be applied in all cases, they do retain historical importance to the development of scientific thought and are still being used today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discovery of microorganisms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Euglena" title="Euglena">Euglena</a></i> that did not fit into either the <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> kingdoms, since they were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> like plants, but <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motile</a> like animals, led to the naming of a third kingdom in the 1860s. In 1860 <a href="/wiki/John_Hogg_(biologist)" title="John Hogg (biologist)">John Hogg</a> called this the Protoctista, and in 1866 <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> named it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protista" title="Protista">Protista</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scamardella1999_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scamardella1999-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild1989_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild1989-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl2005_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl2005-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The work of Pasteur and Koch did not accurately reflect the true diversity of the microbial world because of their exclusive focus on microorganisms having direct medical relevance. It was not until the work of <a href="/wiki/Martinus_Beijerinck" title="Martinus Beijerinck">Martinus Beijerinck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Winogradsky" title="Sergei Winogradsky">Sergei Winogradsky</a> late in the 19th century that the true breadth of microbiology was revealed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brock_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brock-24">[23]</a></sup> Beijerinck made two major contributions to microbiology: the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> and the development of <a href="/wiki/Enrichment_culture" title="Enrichment culture">enrichment culture</a> techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> While his work on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tobacco_Mosaic_Virus" title="Tobacco Mosaic Virus">Tobacco Mosaic Virus</a> established the basic principles of virology, it was his development of enrichment culturing that had the most immediate impact on microbiology by allowing for the cultivation of a wide range of microbes with wildly different physiologies. Winogradsky was the first to develop the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemolithotrophy" title="Chemolithotrophy">chemolithotrophy</a> and to thereby reveal the essential role played by microorganisms in geochemical processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> He was responsible for the first isolation and description of both <a href="/wiki/Nitrifying_bacteria" title="Nitrifying bacteria">nitrifying</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen-fixing_bacteria" title="Nitrogen-fixing bacteria">nitrogen-fixing bacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brock_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brock-24">[23]</a></sup> French-Canadian microbiologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felix_d%27Herelle" title="Felix d'Herelle">Felix d'Herelle</a> co-discovered <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">bacteriophages</a> and was one of the earliest applied microbiologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keen_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keen-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_and_structure">Classification and structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microorganism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Classification and structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Microorganisms can be found almost anywhere on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> are almost always microscopic, while a number of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> are also microscopic, including most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protista" title="Protista">protists</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, as well as some <a href="/wiki/Micro-animal" title="Micro-animal">micro-animals</a> and plants. <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">Viruses</a> are generally regarded as <a href="/wiki/Non-cellular_life" title="Non-cellular life">not living</a> and therefore not considered as microorganisms, although a subfield of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a> is <a href="/wiki/Virology" title="Virology">virology</a>, the study of viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microorganism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_evolution" title="Timeline of evolution">Timeline of evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">Earliest known life forms</a></div>
